<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1235681985">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:var(--background-color-interactive-subtle,#f8f9fa);display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1;min-width:0}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style>
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="skin-invert" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Cuneform_UZ.svg/40px-Cuneform_UZ.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="27" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Cuneform_UZ.svg/60px-Cuneform_UZ.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Cuneform_UZ.svg/80px-Cuneform_UZ.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="630" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support#Cuneiform" title="Help:Multilingual support">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of cuneiform script.</div></div>
</div>
<p><b>Hattusa</b>, also <b>Hattuşa</b>, <b>Ḫattuša</b>, <b>Hattusas</b>, or <b>Hattusha</b>, was the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite Empire</a> in the late <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> during two distinct periods. Its ruins lie near modern <a href="/wiki/Bo%C4%9Fazkale" title="Boğazkale">Boğazkale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, (originally Boğazköy) within the great loop of the <a href="/wiki/K%C4%B1z%C4%B1l%C4%B1rmak_River" title="Kızılırmak River">Kızılırmak River</a> (Hittite: <i>Marashantiya</i>; Greek: <i><a href="/wiki/Halys_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Halys River">Halys</a></i>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Texier" title="Charles Texier">Charles Texier</a> brought attention to the ruins after his visit in 1834. Over the following century, sporadic exploration occurred, involving different archaeologists. In the 20th century, the German Oriental Society and the <a href="/wiki/German_Archaeological_Institute" title="German Archaeological Institute">German Archaeological Institute</a> conducted systematic excavations, which continue to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hattusa was added to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Turkey" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Turkey">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> list in 1986.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hattusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG/220px-Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG/330px-Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG/440px-Hattusa_Yerkapi_rampant.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1880" /></a><figcaption>Hattusa Rampart.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sphinx_Gate,_Hattusa_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/220px-Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/330px-Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/440px-Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>Sphinx Gate entrance of the city.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The earliest traces of settlement on the site are from the sixth millennium BC during the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> period. Toward the end of the 3rd Millennium BC the <a href="/wiki/Hattian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Hattian people">Hattian people</a> established a settlement on locations that had been occupied even earlier and referred to the site as Hattush.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 19th and 18th centuries BC, merchants from <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, centered at <a href="/wiki/Kanesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanesh">Kanesh</a> (Neša) (modern Kültepe) established a trading post there, setting up in their own separate quarter of the lower city.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A carbonized layer apparent in excavations attests to the burning and ruin of the city of Hattusa around 1700 BC. The responsible party appears to have been King <a href="/wiki/Anitta_(king)" title="Anitta (king)">Anitta</a> from <a href="/wiki/Kussara" title="Kussara">Kussara</a>, who took credit for the act and erected an inscribed curse for good measure:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"Whoever after me becomes king resettles Hattusas, let the Stormgod of the Sky strike him!"<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>though in fact the city was rebuilt afterward, possibly by a son of Anitta.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG/220px-Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG/330px-Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG/440px-Hattusa_reconstructed_wall.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1880" /></a><figcaption> Hattusa city walls reconstructed, Hattusa, Turkey.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the first half of the 2nd Millennium BC around the year 1650 BC the Hittite king Labarna moved the capital from Neša to Hattusa and took the name of <a href="/wiki/Hattusili_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Hattusili I">Hattusili</a>, the "one/man from Hattusa".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  After the <a href="/wiki/Kaskians" title="Kaskians">Kaskians</a> arrived to the kingdom's north, they twice attacked the city and under king <a href="/wiki/Tudhaliya_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudhaliya I">Tudhaliya I</a>, the Hittites moved the capital north to <a href="/wiki/Sapinuwa" title="Sapinuwa">Sapinuwa</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Muwatalli_II" title="Muwatalli II">Muwatalli II</a>, they moved south to <a href="/wiki/Tarhuntassa" class="mw-redirect" title="Tarhuntassa">Tarhuntassa</a> but the king assigned his younger brother, the future <a href="/wiki/Hattusili_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Hattusili III">Hattusili III</a> as governor over Hattusa.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the mid-13th century BC Hittite ruler <a href="/wiki/Mursili_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Mursili III">Mursili III</a> returned the seat to Hattusa, where the capital remained until the end of the Hittite kingdom in the 12th century BC (KBo 21.15 i 11–12).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg/170px-Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg/255px-Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg/340px-Hattusa_reliefs1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a><figcaption> Reliefs and Hieroglyphs from Chamber 2 at Hattusa built and decorated by <a href="/wiki/Suppiluliuma_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Suppiluliuma II">Suppiluliuma II</a>, the last king of the Hittites.</figcaption></figure>
<p>At its peak, the city covered 1.8&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (440 acres) and comprised an inner and outer portion, both surrounded by a massive and still visible course of walls erected during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Suppiluliuma_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Suppiluliuma I">Suppiluliuma I</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1344</span>–1322 BC (<a href="/wiki/Short_chronology" class="mw-redirect" title="Short chronology">short chronology</a>)). The inner city covered an area of some 0.8&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (200 acres) and was occupied by a citadel with large administrative buildings and temples. The royal residence, or <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">acropolis</a>, was built on a high ridge now known as Büyükkale (Great Fortress).<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The city displayed over 6&#160;km (3.7&#160;mi) of walls, with inner and outer skins around 3&#160;m (9.8&#160;ft) of thick and 2&#160;m (6&#160;ft 7&#160;in) of space between them, adding 8&#160;m (26&#160;ft) of the total thickness.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hattusa,_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hattusa%2C_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg/220px-Hattusa%2C_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hattusa%2C_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg/330px-Hattusa%2C_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hattusa%2C_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg/440px-Hattusa%2C_capital_of_the_Hittite_Empire_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a><figcaption>The Great Temple in the inner city</figcaption></figure>
<p>To the south lay an outer city of about 1&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (250 acres), with elaborate gateways decorated with reliefs showing warriors, lions, and sphinxes. Four temples were located here, each set around a porticoed courtyard, together with secular buildings and residential structures. Outside the walls are cemeteries, most of which contain cremation burials. Modern estimates put the population of the city around 10,000;<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the early period, the inner city housed a third of that number. The dwelling houses that were built with timber and mud bricks have vanished from the site, leaving only the stone-built walls of temples and palaces.
</p><p>The city was destroyed, together with the Hittite state itself, around 1200 BC, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a>. Excavations suggest that Hattusa was gradually abandoned over a period of several decades as the Hittite empire disintegrated.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been suggested that a regional drought occurred at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Still, signs of final destruction by fire have been noted.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The site was subsequently abandoned until 800 BC, when a modest <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygian</a> settlement appeared in the area.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Archaeology">Archaeology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hattusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Archaeology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg/220px-Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg/330px-Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg/440px-Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1999" data-file-height="1315" /></a><figcaption>Twelve Hittite gods of the Underworld in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Yaz%C4%B1l%C4%B1kaya" title="Yazılıkaya">Yazılıkaya</a>, a sanctuary of Hattusa</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg/220px-%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="118" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg/330px-%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg/440px-%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a_B%C3%BCy%C3%BCkkaya_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5152" data-file-height="2760" /></a><figcaption>Büyükkaya site in Hattusa</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg/170px-Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg/255px-Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg/340px-Museum_Bo%C4%9Fazkale_13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3420" data-file-height="3392" /></a><figcaption>An artifact from the site of Hattusa is presented at Hattusa Museum</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg/220px-Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg/330px-Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg/440px-Hattusa_magazzini_per_cereali.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1759" data-file-height="1192" /></a><figcaption>Large pottery at the excavation site.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1833, the French archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Texier" title="Charles Texier">Félix Marie Charles Texier</a> (1802–1871) was sent on an exploratory mission to Turkey, where in 1834 he discovered monumental ruins near the town of Boğazköy.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Texier made topographical measurements, produced illustrations, and composed a preliminary site plan.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The site was subsequently visited by a number of European travelers and explorers, most notably the German geographer <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Barth" title="Heinrich Barth">Heinrich Barth</a> in 1858.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georges_Perrot" title="Georges Perrot">Georges Perrot</a> excavated at the site in 1861 and at the nearby site of Yazılıkaya.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Perrot was the first to suggest, in 1886, that Boğazköy was the Hittite capital of Hattusa.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1882 German engineer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Humann" title="Carl Humann">Carl Humann</a> completed a full plan of the site.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ernest_Chantre" title="Ernest Chantre">Ernest Chantre</a> opened some trial trenches at the village then called Boğazköy, in 1893–94, with excavations being cut short by a cholera outbreak. Significantly Chantre  discovered some fragments of clay tablets inscribed with cuneiform. The fragments contain text in both the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> and what later was determined to be the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Between 1901 and 1905 <a href="/wiki/Waldemar_Belck" title="Waldemar Belck">Waldemar Belck</a> visited the site several times, finding a number of tablets.
</p><p>In 1905 <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Winckler" title="Hugo Winckler">Hugo Winckler</a> conducted some soundings at Boğazköy on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Orient-Gesellschaft" title="Deutsche Orient-Gesellschaft">German Oriental Society</a> (DOG), finding 35 more cuneiform tablet fragments at the site of the royal fortress, Büyükkale.<sup id="cite_ref-Crüsemann2001_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crüsemann2001-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Winckler began actual excavations in 1906, focusing mainly on the royal fortress area. Thousands of tablets were recovered, most in the then unreadable Hittite language. The few Akkadian texts firmly identified the site as Hattusa.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Winckler returned in 1907 (with <a href="/wiki/Otto_Puchstein" title="Otto Puchstein">Otto Puchstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Kohl" title="Heinrich Kohl">Heinrich Kohl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Curtius" title="Ludwig Curtius">Ludwig Curtius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Krencker" title="Daniel Krencker">Daniel Krencker</a>), and briefly in 1911 and 1912 (with <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Makridi" title="Theodore Makridi">Theodore Makridi</a>). Work stopped with the outbreak of WWI.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tablets from these excavations were published in two series <i>Keilschrifttexte aus Boghazkoi</i> (KB0) and <i>Keilschrift urkunden aus Boghazköi</i> (KUB). Work resumed in 1931 under prehistorian <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Bittel" title="Kurt Bittel">Kurt Bittel</a> with establishing stratigraphy as the major focus. The work was under the auspices of the DOG and <a href="/wiki/German_Archaeological_Institute" title="German Archaeological Institute">German Archaeological Institute</a> (Deutsches Archäologisches Institut) and lasted 9 seasons until being suspended due to the outbreak of WWII in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Excavation resumed in 1952 under Bittel with Peter Neve replacing as field director  in 1963 and as director in 1978, continuing until 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The focus was on the Upper City area. Publication of tablets was resumed in the KUB and KBo.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1994 Jürgen Seeher assumed control of the excavation, leading there until 2005, with the focus on the Büyükkaya and non-monumental areas including economic and residential spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From 2006 on, while some archaeology continued under new director Andreas Schachner, activities have been more focused toward restoration and preparation for tourist operations.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg/170px-Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="302" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg/255px-Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg/340px-Hattusa_Green_Stone_2019-12-08.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2268" data-file-height="4032" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Hattusa_Green_Stone" title="Hattusa Green Stone">Hattusa Green Stone</a>, a monument believed to have religious origins</figcaption></figure>
<p>During the 1986 excavations a large (35 × 24&#160;cm, 5&#160;kg in weight, with 2 attached chains) inscribed metal tablet was discovered 35 meters west of the Sphinx Gate. The tablet, from the 13th century BC, contained a treaty between Hittite <a href="/wiki/Tud%E1%B8%ABaliya_IV" title="Tudḫaliya IV">Tudḫaliya IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurunta" title="Kurunta">Kurunta</a>, King of <a href="/wiki/Tar%E1%B8%ABunta%C5%A1%C5%A1a" title="Tarḫuntašša">Tarḫuntašša</a>. It is held at the Museum of Anatolian Civilizations in Ankara.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During 1991 repair work at the site a Mycenae bronze sword was found on the western slope. It was inscribed, in Akkadian, "As Duthaliya the Great King shattered the Assuwa-Country he dedicated these swords to the Storm-God, his lord".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another significant find during the 1990-91 excavation season in the "Westbau" building of the upper city, was 3400 sealed bullae and clay lumps dating from the 2nd half of the 13th century BC. They were primarily associated with land documents.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cuneiform_royal_archives">Cuneiform royal archives</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hattusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cuneiform royal archives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg/200px-Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="336" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg/300px-Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg/400px-Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2154" data-file-height="3617" /></a><figcaption>Treaty of Kadesh</figcaption></figure>
<p>Forty mercantile documents written in the Old Assyrian dialect of Akkadian were found in the early 2nd millennium BC karum. By the middle of the 2nd millennium a scribal community had grown up in Hattusa based on Syrian, Mesopotamian, and Hurrian input. This included the usual range of Akkadian and Sumerian language texts.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most important discoveries at the site has been the cuneiform royal archives of clay tablets from the Hittite Empire New Kingdom period, known as the <a href="/wiki/Bogazk%C3%B6y_Archive" title="Bogazköy Archive">Bogazköy Archive</a>, consisting of official correspondence and contracts, as well as legal codes, procedures for cult ceremony, oracular prophecies and literature of the ancient Near East. <a href="/wiki/Egyptian%E2%80%93Hittite_peace_treaty" title="Egyptian–Hittite peace treaty">One particularly important tablet</a>, currently on display at the <a href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museum">Istanbul Archaeology Museum</a>, details the terms of a peace settlement reached years after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kadesh" title="Battle of Kadesh">Battle of Kadesh</a> between the Hittites and the Egyptians under <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a>, in 1259 or 1258 BC. A copy is on display in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> as an example of the earliest known international peace treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although the 30,000 or so clay tablets recovered from Hattusa form the main corpus of Hittite literature, archives have since appeared at other centers in Anatolia, such as <a href="/wiki/Tabigga" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabigga">Tabigga</a> (Maşat Höyük) and Sapinuwa (Ortaköy).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sphinxes">Sphinxes</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hattusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sphinxes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sphinx_Gate,_Hattusa_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/220px-Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/330px-Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/440px-Sphinx_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>Sphinx Gate entrance of the city.</figcaption></figure>
<p>A pair of <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">sphinxes</a> found at the southern gate in Hattusa were taken for restoration to <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> in 1917. The better-preserved was returned to Turkey in 1924 and placed on display in the <a href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museums">Istanbul Archaeology Museum</a>, but the other remained in Germany where it was on display at the <a href="/wiki/Pergamon_Museum" title="Pergamon Museum">Pergamon Museum</a> from 1934 until it was moved to the Boğazköy Museum outside the Hattusa ruins<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2023)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, along with the Istanbul sphinx reuniting the pair near their original location.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
